# Learning Functional Programming with JavaScript ES6+

> Want to write more robust, maintainable code? In this course, discover how this can be accomplished with functional programming, and learn about the functional concepts at the heart of many JavaScript frameworks and programs. Functional programming is a radically different way of writing code. While at first it may seem a bit counterintuitive, once you get comfortable with it, you may find that yo

### 1. Introduction

#### Functional programming will change the way you think about code
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/functional-programming-will-change-the-way-you-think-about-code?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/functional-programming-will-change-the-way-you-think-about-code?u=76281980&t=0)** - Writing code using functional programming concepts is the single most important approach you can take to ensure your applications are both maintainable and scalable.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/functional-programming-will-change-the-way-you-think-about-code?u=76281980&t=8)** An increasing number of developers are switching from object-oriented programming to functional programming as a way to minimize the potential for bugs in their code while maximizing its readability and reusability.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/functional-programming-will-change-the-way-you-think-about-code?u=76281980&t=21)** I'm Shaun Wassell, and in this course I'll show you the basics of functional programming in JavaScript.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/functional-programming-will-change-the-way-you-think-about-code?u=76281980&t=26)** So let's jump right in and see how to take your JavaScript code to a whole new level.

> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=82)** If you fit that description, then this course is definitely for you.

### 2. 1. Introductory Functional Concepts

#### The goal of functional programming
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] So, the big question most people ask when they hear about how useful functional programming is is, "What exactly is functional programming?"
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=7)** Well, to answer this question, it might be helpful to consider the question of why we would want to use functional programming in the first place.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=15)** In other words, what are the problems that functional programming sets out to solve?
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=20)** Well, if you currently use an object-oriented programming language, such as Java or C++, or especially a procedural programming language such as C, you've probably run into this problem before.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=31)** There are certain bugs that are difficult to track down and fix, partly because they're hard to recreate, it takes a long, convoluted series of steps to make them show up, and partly because even when you figure out how to recreate them, it's next to impossible for you to keep track of all the changes that occur while the program is running.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=49)** In a typical enterprise sized program, thousands of variables are being operated on in thousands of different places at runtime, and the application can get itself into buggy states, which can be difficult to recreate.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=62)** This is exactly the type of situation that functional programming aims to avoid.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=67)** Once you learn to think and program in a functional style, this type of situation will become much less frequent.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=73)** You'll learn many techniques that can be used to create very large, powerful, and virtually bug-free code bases that are composed of many smaller, self-contained, and easily testable parts.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=84)** So then, at its core, functional programming is concerned with taking a large number of complex ideas present in any large computer program and organizing them in a coherent way, while at the same time making sure that the code remains easy to test and modify.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=99)** Of course, this sort of organization is what object-oriented programming aims to do as well, although it achieves it with only varied success.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=106)** The difference between object-oriented and functional programming is in the choices they make with regards to organizing code.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=113)** As you probably know already, the basic idea behind object-oriented programming is that humans think in terms of objects and relationships between these objects, and therefore computer programs should be organized in terms of objects so that we can leverage our existing thought patterns to write programs more easily.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=130)** The problem with this, of course, is that our thought patterns are often not as well defined as we sometimes like to think.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=137)** Once we go beyond contrived examples, such as SUV is a subclass of car, car is a subclass of vehicle, which demonstrates the concept but isn't very helpful, the object-oriented programming doctrine doesn't provide much guidance regarding code organization.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=152)** We're left with a rather daunting array of concepts like interfaces, subclasses, constructors, public, private, and protected data, accessors, mutators, and method overriding, all of which form a complicated web of relationships and are prone to abuse by all but the most experienced and disciplined programmers.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=171)** On the other hand, functional programming aims to bring the precision of mathematical functions into computer programs.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=177)** While there will always be room for human error in programming, no matter what paradigm is being used, adding the provable certainty that comes with mathematics into computer programming makes it far easier to avoid bugs.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=189)** Imagine if we were able to represent all parts of a computer program as simply as a mathematical function, such as function of X equals x plus one.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=197)** Where could a bug possibly be hiding in functions like this?
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=201)** We'll look further into the details of functional programming along with the many differences between it and object-oriented programming throughout the rest of the course.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/the-goal-of-functional-programming?u=76281980&t=209)** Since many of you are probably most familiar with object-oriented programming, I'll continue to use it in a lot of comparisons with functional programming to clarify the concepts I show you.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (2), self (1), this, (1), public (1), private (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (4), imagine (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), in other words (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Env Vars:** suv (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)

#### Declarative vs. imperative programming
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the first things you'll want to understand about functional programming is that it's a declarative style of programming as opposed to things like object-oriented programming and procedural programming, which are imperative styles of programming.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=12)** What this means is that functional programming puts more focus on what things are as opposed to how to get them.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=19)** A simple example of this would be if we were to talk about houses.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=22)** If we wanted to describe a house in a declarative way, if we wanted to describe what a house is, we might say that a house is a foundation, four walls and a roof on top.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=33)** On the other hand, if we were describing a house in an imperative way, if we wanted to describe how to make a house, we might say, in order to make a house, you need to pour the foundation, build four walls, and then put a roof on top.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=47)** Let's expand on this a bit and look at a more programming related example.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=51)** Let's say that we want to write a program that tells us the average of an array of numbers.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=55)** Imperative programming would specify the steps required to calculate the result, something like this.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=61)** First, we set X equal to zero.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=63)** Second, we add the first number in the array to X, and then we repeat step two for the rest of the numbers in the array and finally, we divide X by the length of the array.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=75)** Now, unless you've worked with functional programming before, this is probably how you're used to writing programs, thinking of how to get the result.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=83)** On the other hand, if we wanted to solve the same problem in a declarative way, we could simply say that X is the sum of all the numbers in the array divided by the length of the array.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=93)** Or if you're more mathematically inclined, we could rewrite it using mathematical notation like this.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=99)** Now, if it never occurred to you that it's possible to write programs in this way, by simply stating what something is, you're not alone.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=106)** You probably notice that talking about things declaratively, talking about what they are, instead of how to get them, feels a lot more natural and well, frankly, it's a lot easier for many problems.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=117)** This way of talking about things may also remind some of you of mathematical functions such as these, and as I mentioned before, this is one of the main ideas behind functional programming to bring the precision of mathematical functions into our programs.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=131)** As you may have figured, this is also where functional programming gets its name.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=135)** So it's all very easy to say that functional programming brings the precision of mathematics into programming.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=141)** But what does that mean exactly?
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=143)** Well, there are three main concepts behind functional programming which allow us to do just that.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=147)** These concepts are one, immutability, two, separating functions and data, and three first-class functions.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/declarative-vs-imperative-vs-programming?u=76281980&t=156)** Over the course of the next few videos, I'll go into depth on what each of these concepts means, contrast it with how other paradigms do things and explain its advantages.

> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)

#### Immutability
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The first major concept of functional programming is immutability, and it's a concept that may surprise a lot of people at first.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=7)** Most programmers learned early on that you could assign a value to a variable.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=11)** For example, we can define a variable called X and store the value five in it, and then later on in the program, we can change the value of that variable to some completely different number, and later on we can change its value again, and so on.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=25)** However, in functional programming, this is not allowed.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=28)** When we say that X is equal to five, we mean that for the rest of the program, X will only ever be five.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=33)** There's no way we can change it.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=36)** In short, immutability means that most of the values in a program are constant, which in JavaScript means using the constant keyword instead of the var or let keywords.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=46)** Another way to think about it is this.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=49)** In object-oriented and procedural programming, we treat variables as buckets that we can put values into.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=55)** We call this assigning values to variables.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=58)** So at one point in time, X might hold the value three.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=61)** At another point it might hold the value 10.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=64)** At another point, it might be negative four, and so on.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=67)** On the other hand, in functional programming, we don't assign, we define.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=71)** Instead of naming buckets that hold different values at different times, we name the values themselves.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=77)** When we say that X equals three, we don't mean that X is a container that's currently holding the value three.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=83)** We literally mean that X is three, in the same way that Pi is 3.14159 et cetera.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=90)** Could you imagine changing the value of Pi?
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=92)** Of course not.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=93)** Functional programming treats all values as if they were as concrete and unchanging as Pi or any other mathematical constant.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=100)** And so once we've defined X, for the whole entire program, X can never be anything else.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=106)** It's just another name for whatever value we've defined it as.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=110)** At first glance, this may seem strange to most programmers.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=113)** After all, what good is a program where you can't change anything?
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=116)** How can a program like that be useful at all?
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=119)** Let's look at a quick example.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=121)** What if in a certain theoretical program we have an employee and we want to raise their salary?
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=127)** Well, in object-oriented programming, of course, we can just change it directly, usually by calling a member function like this.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=135)** In functional programming, on the other hand, we would instead define a new constant that represents the updated data and then use this constant in our future calculations.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=144)** Notice here also that I'm not using the new keyword as we do an object oriented programming.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=149)** I'm just defining our data in a JavaScript object.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=151)** We'll learn more about why this is later on.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=155)** The advantage of immutability and the reason that functional programming places such an emphasis on it is that it frees us from having to deal with state change.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=163)** When a program contains many variables that are all changing constantly at different times, it can be very hard to know what state a program is in at any given point in time.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=172)** As programs increase in size to include thousands or even millions of individual variables, this can lead to hard-to-find bugs and an overall fragile code base that programmers are afraid to make changes to.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=184)** This is a problem that even test-driven development can't solve completely, since the task of testing all possible states that a program might get itself into is nearly impossible in programs of considerable size.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=196)** In functional programming, on the other hand, we start off with an immutable set of data as a single source of truth, and then we use functions to combine this data piece by piece, and transform it into useful information.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=209)** This data is usually retrieved from a database or some other memory storage.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=213)** There are two powerful advantages to this approach.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=216)** The first is that the original data in a program will always remain intact, which makes bugs much easier to find.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=223)** The second is that programs constructed in this way are much easier to keep track of, since you can focus on any given piece individually.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=231)** The only thing that determines the output of a given piece is the input.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=236)** We don't have to think about the entire system all the time.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=239)** If the concept of immutability doesn't quite make sense to you yet, or if the advantages don't seem apparent, don't worry.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=246)** Later on in the course, we'll take a look at many examples that incorporate immutability.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/immutability?u=76281980&t=251)** For now, just remember that in functional programming, all data is immutable, so use constant instead of let or var.

> **CLI Commands:** find (2), make (2)

#### Separation of data and functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The second main concept of functional programming is the separation of data and functions.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=5)** This is a case where functional programming is directly in contrast with object-oriented programming, where data and functions are almost always grouped together.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=13)** Let's see what this looks like.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=15)** First of all, for our purposes, let's discuss what data and functions actually are.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=20)** For our purposes, data might be any values that a program contains.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=23)** People's names, addresses, and social security numbers in a payroll program.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=27)** The models years, and colors of cars on a used car website.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=31)** The positions, health levels, and weapons of characters in a video game, anything.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=36)** All of this is data.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=37)** In object-oriented programming, this data is usually wrapped up inside objects as member variables, and the only way we can access it is using an object's methods.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=47)** In functional programming on the other hand, this data is represented by simple arrays and hashes, or in this case, JavaScript objects.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=55)** And a function is any operation that we can apply to our data to convert it into useful information.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=61)** For example, if we want to find the average salary of programmers at our company, find all the cars on our website that were made after a certain year, or find if two characters are colliding in our video game.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=73)** In object-oriented programming, functions are wrapped up inside objects along with the data that they operate on, and allow us to access or make changes to that data.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=84)** In functional programming on the other hand, functions are completely separate entities from the data that they operate on.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=90)** In order to operate on given data, the data must be passed as arguments to the function instead of using the this keyword as in object-oriented programming.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=99)** And because of the rule of immutability, they should never make changes to any of the data they touch.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/separation-of-data-and-functions?u=76281980&t=104)** They only return a modified copy of that data.

#### Converting object-oriented to functional code
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=0)** - If you're coming from an object-oriented background, it may seem strange to you that functional programming places an emphasis on keeping data and functions separate.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=8)** Since one of the central concepts of object-oriented programming is that we put data and the functions related to that data together in objects.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=16)** To understand why it makes sense to keep data and functions separate and functional programming, it might be helpful to think about why we keep them together in object-oriented programming in the first place.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=27)** The main reason that it's useful to keep data and functions together in the same object is that this allows us to interact with the member variables of an object without touching them directly.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=37)** What's wrong with touching variables directly?
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=39)** Well, if we give programmers direct access to variables, they can, and in my experience, will change them in ways that they're not supposed to.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=47)** Consider the simple case of a person object with publicly accessible properties, first name, last name, and initials.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=54)** Now, if we allow programmers to change these properties directly, they have to remember to reset initials every time they change either first name or last name, which they're almost certain to forget somewhere, which introduces bugs in the program.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=67)** The most popular solution to this is to make the variables private, which in JavaScript is usually done by just adding an underscore before the variable name and telling developers not to touch it, and then we allow programmers access to the variables only through functions, which make sure that the variables are kept up to date.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=86)** In functional programming however, we don't need to worry about making all our data private because programmers can't alter the data in unwanted ways.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=93)** Because of the rule of immutability, which we discussed in the previous video, the data remains a constant source of truth.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=99)** Note that in JavaScript we need a little extra help to keep developers from modifying our object properties, but we'll talk about that in the coming video.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=108)** If we want to transform the data in some way, such as changing someone's name, we simply define a new constant that represents the updated data.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=116)** We then use this updated constant in all our future calculations knowing exactly what data it represents.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=123)** Let's take a look at a simple example to demonstrate how functional programming and object-oriented programming handle functions and data differently.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=130)** Let's imagine the classic example of creating a simple to-do list program that helps people keep track of tasks they need to complete.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=138)** Let's look at what an object-oriented approach to this program would look like.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=142)** First, we'd create a to-do item class that contains properties such as the name, category, and deadline for the item and whether or not it's been completed, as well as functions such as change, name or mark as done, and then of course, our getters to access our private variables.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=158)** Then we might have another class called to-do list, which contains an array of to-do items as well as functions for adding or removing items or sorting them in different ways.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=167)** In functional programming on the other hand, we'd keep the data and functions separate.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=172)** Remember that in functional programming, instead of wrapping up our data's properties as member variables and side classes, we usually represent data using constructs such as arrays or hashes, which in JavaScript, somewhat confusingly, we call objects.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=188)** So for this program, we might have JavaScript objects that represent to-do items with name, category, and deadline properties, and the list itself, which we would simply represent with an array that holds a bunch of to-do items.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/converting-object-oriented-to-functional-code?u=76281980&t=200)** Then outside of these classes, we define the functions for combining or transforming our data, such as sorting it or marking an item as done.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Code Keywords:** private (3), let (3)
> **Analogies:** such as (3), imagine (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Cross-References:** we discussed (1), previous video (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (1)
> **Speakers:** - if (1)

#### First-class functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The third major concept of functional programming is something called first-class functions.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=5)** This is where things really start to get interesting and where the power and flexibility of functional programming really starts to shine through.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=12)** So what exactly do we mean when we talk about first-class functions?
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=16)** Well, in object-oriented programming, we generally treat data and functions as different types of entities.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=23)** We would probably never think of, for example, creating an array of functions or passing functions as arguments to other functions, or even stranger, returning functions from other functions.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=34)** However, in functional programming, it's not only possible to do these things, it's, in fact, a source of tremendous flexibility.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=41)** This is exactly what we mean when we say that a language supports first-class functions.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=45)** It allows us to treat functions in a very similar way to how we normally treat other values, such as numbers, strings, and objects.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=53)** But hold on a minute.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=54)** Doesn't this go against what I said in the previous video about the separation of data and functions in functional programming?
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=60)** Well, at first glance, it might seem like it, so let me clarify.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=64)** In object-oriented programming, since functions and data are both defined within the same class, they tend to be tightly coupled.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=71)** In other words, the functions inside a given class usually reference the data that they operate on directly, as is the case here with this._name.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=80)** In other words, you generally can't take a given function and remove it from the class it's in and still have it work on its own.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=87)** In functional programming, on the other hand, we design our functions specifically so that they work in isolation.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=93)** All of the data that a function needs to operate on is passed in through the arguments.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=98)** And the only thing that will change the output of the function is a change in the arguments.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=103)** In other words, functions aren't influenced by any kind of internal or external state.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=108)** There's a name for this kind of function.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=110)** We call them pure functions, and in functional programming, we try our best to make every single one of our functions pure.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=116)** And this is what we mean when we say that we want to keep data and functions separate.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=121)** Now, you might be wondering why we'd want to do stuff like this.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=125)** What could we possibly gain from doing weird things like having arrays of functions or passing functions as arguments?
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=131)** In fact, this simple change in mindset toward functions opens up a world of possibilities in terms of software design.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=138)** It gives us a lot of flexibility and greatly increases our opportunities for code reuse.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=143)** It also allows us to do useful things like combine existing functions to create new functions.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/first-class-functions?u=76281980&t=148)** We'll see in the next chapter how this is done and what it's implications are for our programs.

#### Ensuring immutability with ESLint
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So I talked in a previous video about the importance of immutability in functional programming.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=5)** Immutability allows us to avoid the many bugs that occur in computer programs as a side effect of state change.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=11)** It also means that we don't have to worry about using private variables to avoid changes coming from unexpected places, since we simply can't make changes to any piece of data.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=21)** That being said, if we want to ensure complete immutability in JavaScript, there's something we need to keep in mind.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=27)** While using the const keyword prevents us from directly changing the value of a piece of data, for example, if we say const X equals five, we can't simply say X equals six, since this will throw an error.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=41)** However, using constant JavaScript is a little funny when working with arrays and objects.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=46)** For example, if we define an array called numbers, like this, constant numbers 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, we can never directly redefine numbers as a different array like this, numbers equals hello.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=66)** But we can modify the individual elements of an array like this.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=70)** Numbers zero equals 100.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=75)** And JavaScript won't complain about this at all, even though we've defined our numbers array using the const keyword.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=81)** Also, if we're not careful, we can accidentally call a mutating array method such as reverse on a supposedly immutable array like this.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=89)** Numbers reverse, and this will actually modify the original array without any complaint from JavaScript.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=98)** And the same is true for objects as well.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=99)** If we define an object called person, const person, with name and age properties, name John Doe, age 34, we can never directly redefine person as another object like this, person equals name Bob.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=121)** But if we try and reset a property of our object like this, person.name equals Bob, JavaScript won't complain even though this is modifying our data.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=132)** Now I'm going to show you an approach that we can use to prevent mutation in our code, and this is by using ESLint rules.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=140)** If you've never worked with or heard of ESLint before, it's an extremely helpful tool that helps you catch errors or stylistic flaws in your code.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=148)** ESLint won't add anything extra to your code.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=151)** We can simply run it and it looks at our existing code and tells us any problems it finds, for example, if we're doing accidental mutation.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-with-eslint?u=76281980&t=159)** Now there are many different ESLint plugins that we can use depending on our coding style and concerns.

> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=75)** So now, if we delete all this code and rewrite some code that we might find when we're accidentally mutating our data, like this.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=83)** const person equals, name, John Doe, and age equals 34.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=93)** And then we try and mutate the name property.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=95)** person.name equals Bob.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=99)** And then we save our code.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=101)** And then if we go into our terminal and type npx eslint, and then the directory that we want to run lint on, in this case, just the current directory we're in.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=111)** So we'll use dot, and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=115)** We see that in addition to a few other lint errors, ESLint also gives us an error saying that no object mutation is allowed.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=123)** Keep in mind also that if you don't want to use the default rules from Airbnb, you can go into your eslintrc and simply delete extends: 'airbnb-base' from the object.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=135)** But I'm going to leave it in there.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=137)** So there you have it.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/ensuring-immutability-finalize-eslint?u=76281980&t=138)** When we're creating a functional codebase and want to ensure that we don't accidentally mutate any of our data, we can set up ESLint to catch any mutations for us.

>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=454)** We'll talk about that in more detail shortly.
>
> **[7:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=457)** The third way is using arrow functions with a full function body and the return keyword.
>
> **[7:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=462)** The fourth way is omitting the return keyword, if you have a short enough function body.
>
> **[7:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=468)** The fifth way is if you have only one argument, you can remove those parentheses around the arguments.
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=474)** The sixth way... Actually, this is just sort of a repeat of what we had up there, so we'll count this as number six here.
>
> **[8:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=480)** The sixth way is if you're returning an object from an arrow function and you're using this shorter syntax that we saw up here, you need to put parentheses around the object that you're returning.
>
> **[8:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=490)** Alright, so there's really six or seven different ways that you can keep in mind here to write functions in JavaScript.
>
> **[8:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=497)** And the last thing I wanted to point out here is what makes this first function syntax significantly different from the other syntaxes here.
>
> **[8:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=506)** There's one key difference, and that is that functions that are defined this first way are hoisted, right?
>
> **[8:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=514)** There's a word in JavaScript called function hoisting.
>
> **[8:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=517)** And basically what that means is if we define this function anywhere in the file, then we can use it before the function definition, right?
>
> **[8:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=527)** So we could still say myFunction(1, 2) for example, up here at the top, and it would work.
>
> **[8:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=533)** Now, that is the only function definition that we're allowed to do that with.
>
> **[8:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=536)** If we were to do that with any of these other functions here, because these functions are defined as variables, those functions wouldn't be hoisted and we would get an error.
>
> **[9:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/javascript-arrow-function-syntax?u=76281980&t=546)** Alright, so anyway, this has been an in-depth look at how to define functions in JavaScript, and the many different syntaxes for doing so.

#### Using functions as data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so now that we've taken an in-depth look at the syntax or syntaxes, plural, of defining functions in JavaScript, let's move on to the next topic.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980&t=11)** And that is, I mentioned in a previous video that functional programming treats functions as first class citizens, meaning that we can treat functions in a very similar way to other types, such as strings, numbers, or objects.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980&t=24)** So what I'm going to do here, first of all, is create a new file.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980&t=27)** And we'll call this something like functions as data.js.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980&t=33)** All right, so what we're going to do here is we're going to take a look at what this concept of first class functions means for our code and a few useful things that we can do with it.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980&t=42)** Now, the easiest way to demonstrate how first class functions can be used like other data types, is with the following example.
>

>
> **[8:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980&t=503)** All right, so the idea here is that if we just exported this fetch data function, then the rest of our application could use this fetchData function without worrying about what the implementation was, whether it was the fake version or the real version.
>
> **[8:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-functions-as-data?u=76281980&t=519)** So this is just one example of something that's available to us in functional programming just by changing our mindset around what functions are and treating them like we treat other data types.

>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/creating-arrays-of-functions?u=76281980&t=375)** So when we just want to create an array of functions, or as we saw previously when we just wanted to assign a function to a constant, we can simply use the function's name without any parentheses after it.

> **[9:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/returning-functions?u=76281980&t=599)** All right, so now by making that single change, we've been able to make that change to this entire family of functions, and if we run our code now, what we'll see is that, sure enough, we see that printed out there.
>
> **[10:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/returning-functions?u=76281980&t=611)** This is a little bit of a simplistic example, of course, but with more complex situations, this kind of thing, this ability to create families of functions like this, is an incredibly powerful concept.
>
> **[10:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/returning-functions?u=76281980&t=626)** We'll see some examples of this later on in the course.

> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=124)** And then maybe we'll just return nothing there.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=128)** Okay, so, this works, and this kind of argument checking is pretty common in many codebases.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=136)** But the problem with this is that we're cluttering up our divide function here with argument checking logic, Right?
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=146)** Ideally, each function should really only have one main concern.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=150)** And in the case of our divide function, we don't want it to have to worry about checking its own arguments.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=155)** That really is kind of cluttering up our function and obscuring the real job of this function down here.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=162)** So here's our basic plan of attack here.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=165)** What we're going to do is we're going to create another function that we'll be able to use to sort of wrap our divide function and provide this argument-checking logic for us.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=180)** That might sound a little bit confusing and a little bit vague.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=182)** So let me show you what this is going to look like.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=185)** What we're going to do here is we're going to create a new function here.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=189)** We'll call this something like secondArgZeroCheck, all right, something like that.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=197)** And what this is going to do is it's actually going to take a function as an argument, and it's going to make sure that that function won't be called if the second argument is 0.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=212)** So here's what this is going to look like.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=214)** We're going to return a function from inside of here.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=218)** Actually, here we'll just return a function the arrow function way.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=221)** And I'm going to do something here that's a little bit confusing that I'll talk about later.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=226)** So in case you haven't seen this syntax before, don't worry too much about it.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=229)** This is just called the spread operator.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=232)** And basically what it does is it gives us any and all of the arguments that were passed into the function as an array.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=240)** All right, so it's a nice way to allow the calling code to pass in however many args it wants.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=245)** So now that we've done this, what we're going to do is, inside this function that we're returning, this is where we're going to write the argument-checking logic.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=254)** So essentially we're just going to say if, and we're going to duplicate what we had up here, in fact, I can just copy this and paste it there like so.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=263)** We do have to change this to args[1], right?
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=267)** Getting the second arg there, and we'll change this message as well, since our secondArgZeroCheck function doesn't really know what we want to do with this.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=277)** We're just going to say something like, 'Error! Second arg cannot be zero!'
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=284)** All right.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=285)** And that's what happens if the argument is 0, we're just going to log out that error and return.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=290)** Otherwise, what we're going to do is we're just going to call the function that we passed in originally with whatever these args happen to be.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=299)** So we're going to say return function, called with all of the arguments, we have to use the dot, dot dot before that here again as well, in order to actually pass the contents of an array as arguments instead of the array itself.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=314)** And now, what we can do is we can remove this logic from our divide function, bringing it back to a nice simple function that it was before.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=325)** And here's how we can actually use these two functions together to provide the same exact functionality that our divide function had before when it was doing its own argument checking.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=337)** What we're going to do is we're just going to say const, and we'll call this something like divideSafe, equals, and now we're just going to pass our divide function to secondArgZeroCheck by saying secondArgZeroCheck(divide).
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=355)** And now, this divideSafe function that we have is going to have pretty much the exact same behavior as what we saw earlier when our divide function had this logic inside of it.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=365)** So let's test this out.
