> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=367)** We're going to say console.log(divideSafe), and let's try passing in two numbers that are valid here, 10 and 2.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=375)** And let's actually also pass in, we'll say divideSafe, and then we'll say 10 and 0.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=382)** So let's see how this thing behaves there.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=384)** What we're going to see is if we run our code with node examples/higher-order.js, sure enough, we see that the first one returns 5 and the second one actually displays that error saying second arg cannot be zero and returns undefined.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=401)** All right, now, it might not be immediately clear how this fits together.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=405)** So I would highly recommend that you walk through this visually.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=409)** You know, when calling divideSafe.
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=411)** Remember that when we pass a function as an argument, all we're doing is just passing any call to function with whatever that function was.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=420)** So here we're just saying return divide(...args), right?
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=425)** And if we pass in 10 and 2 here, we're just replacing args with 10 and 2.
>
> **[7:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=431)** All right?
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=432)** And the rest of the stuff, right, this if statement, this is going to get called now every time we call this divideSafe thing, and it'll provide a guard around this divide thing, meaning that divide isn't going to get called if this second argument is 0.
>
> **[7:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=449)** So anyway, that's the basic idea of higher-order functions.
>
> **[7:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=453)** And just like how we saw that returning functions could be used to create sort of families of functions, higher-order functions can be used to actually add extra functionality onto existing functions without modifying the functions themselves.
>
> **[7:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=471)** And this is a really powerful idea.
>
> **[7:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/higher-order-functions?u=76281980&t=476)** So anyway, that is the basics of higher-order functions in JavaScript.

### 4. 3. JavaScript Array Manipulation

#### Basics of functional data manipulation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so now that we've taken a look at first class functions, which as you've seen, give you a lot of power and flexibility in your programs, the next thing that we're going to take a look at here is the basics of manipulating data in a functional way, right?
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=16)** So in other words, when you have some data and you want to modify it, how do you do that in functional programming?
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=23)** Well, let's start off by taking a look at the procedural way of making these kinds of changes.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=29)** In the procedural way, we generally write our programs in such a way that we're specifying how to calculate the result, right?
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=39)** So in other words, we're specifying a process or procedure, hence procedural, for how to calculate the result that we're looking for.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=48)** And this generally involves a series of steps.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=51)** First, we're going to initialize a variable, then we're going to loop through all of the elements in an array, and this is usually done using a for loop, and generally that for loop will modify the variable that we initialized, so that once we're done with the loop, that variable is going to contain the result.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=72)** Now, the problem with this is that between the step where we initialize the variable and when we're done and have the result, that variable is kind of in this uncomfortable intermediate point where it's not really the result, but it's also not what we initialized it as.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=92)** And this can make our programs pretty difficult to troubleshoot if something doesn't work the way we want it to, as I'm sure you've experienced when working with things like complex or nested loops.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=104)** So, the functional way of manipulating data, I really think you're going to like this way a lot better.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=111)** The first thing to know about the functional way of manipulating data is that instead of specifying how to calculate the result and the steps required to calculate the result, we simply write our programs in such a way that it tells JavaScript what it is that we want.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=128)** That might sound a little bit strange, you'll see exactly what this looks like very shortly.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=134)** But generally it means that we don't use any for loops, right?
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=137)** So instead of for loops, we're generally going to use a number of other functions with names like map, filter, reduce, and sort.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=147)** And these functions will allow us to get the exact same result and apply the exact same operations as we were able to do with a for loop, but in a much more functional and declarative way.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=162)** Now, we'll get into these functions shortly, but one thing that I want you to remember here before we get into these functions is that with some of these functions, you might find yourself wondering how the function is actually working, right?
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=175)** What process is actually going on behind the scenes with a lot of these functions?
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=180)** For example, with the sort function, you might wonder, is the function using quick sort, merge sort?
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=186)** You know, what kind of algorithm is it using behind the scenes?
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=189)** Well, I'm going to recommend that you do your best to not worry about how these things actually work behind the scenes, right?
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=196)** That's not really the point.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=197)** The point of these functions is to allow you to express what you want instead of how to calculate what you want in a declarative way.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=209)** So again, just keep that in mind.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/basics-of-functional-data-manipulation?u=76281980&t=211)** We'll see what this looks like in much more detail when we jump into these functions, which we're going to do right now.

#### Using the map() function
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, so now that we've taken a look at the spread operator, let's move on to the first of several built-in functions that we're going to see that will really help us manipulate data in a functional or declarative way.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=15)** So the first of these functions is called map, and here's the basics of how the map function works.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=22)** Essentially, the map function allows us to take an array full of elements and apply some sort of transformation to each and every element in the array.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=33)** So for example, we might have an array of numbers and we might want to double all of the numbers.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=38)** Now, another example of this might be if we have an array of measurements that are in inches, and we might want to convert all of those measurements to centimeter measurements.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=48)** Well, that's another thing that we could use the map function for.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=52)** And we might also have, let's say, an array of objects representing people and we might want an array containing just those people's names.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=63)** Well, this is yet another case where we could use the map function.

> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=69)** Now, the thing to know about this, and we'll take a look at this in just a minute, is that the general way that this is done in procedural programming is just using a for loop to loop through all of the elements and apply that transformation.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=83)** But again, that's a very procedural way to go about doing things and generally can be very difficult to troubleshoot when something goes wrong.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=93)** So that is where the map function comes in, and the general way that the map function is used, here's where what we learned in the previous chapter about passing functions as arguments is going to come in handy.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=105)** The way that this map function is used is we can simply define a function representing the transformation that we want to take place, and we can pass that to map, right?
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=117)** So if we have an array of numbers, let's say, we can just say numbers.map and pass any function to that, and the resulting array will be an array containing the results of calling that function with each element in the array.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=135)** Now that might sound a little bit confusing still.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=137)** So just to give you an idea of how this might work, if we wanted to square all of the elements in an array, here's how we would do it.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=145)** We would just say numbers.map, and then you see we have an arrow function here with x that takes x, which represents each individual element in the array, and it returns x times itself, the result being an array containing all of those numbers squared.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-map-function?u=76281980&t=164)** So that's the basic idea of how this map function works.

#### Using the every() and some() functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, well, at this point we've talked about the map function and the filter function, so the next function that we're going to take a look at is actually two functions, and these are the every and some functions.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=12)** Now these two functions are kind of similar to each other and they're also kind of similar to the filter function, but with every and some, what we want to do generally is take an array of some sort of data, right?
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=26)** Numbers, or strings, or objects, something like that.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=29)** It can be anything.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=31)** And we want to get a single true or false answer regarding whether the elements in the array match some sort of criteria.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=40)** So to give you an idea of what this might look like, let's say that we have an array of all odd numbers, one, three, five, seven, and nine, and we want to know whether all of those numbers are odd.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=52)** Well, in that case, what we would do is we would define a function called isOdd that tells us whether or not a single number is odd, and we would pass that to numbers.every.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=64)** Now, in this case, it would return true since every number is odd, right?
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=69)** It reads almost like a sentence there.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=71)** However, if any of those numbers doesn't fit the criteria, in this case, if at least one of the numbers is even or not odd, then it will return false, right?
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=86)** So if all of the numbers are odd, then it returns true, if any of the numbers are not odd, it returns false.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=93)** And of course, if all of the elements are not odd, it returns false as well.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=100)** So that's the every function.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=101)** The some function behaves in a similar way, but there's one key difference to this.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=108)** The some function checks to see whether any of the elements in the array returns true for the function that we pass, whereas every checks to see whether all of the elements return true for that function.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=122)** So going back to our array of all odd numbers here, if we say numbers.some isOdd for this array, that's going to return true, right?
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=131)** Because there's at least one element in there that is odd.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=136)** Now here's where things get a little bit different between some and every.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=139)** In the case where we have one element that's not odd, right?
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=144)** If we have an even number in there, well, some is still going to return true here because there's still at least one element that returns true.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=152)** Whereas with every, it would've started returning false by now.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=156)** And this continues all the way up until we have not a single element in the array that returns true, at which point numbers.some will return false.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=169)** All right?
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=170)** So what you see here is the main similarity between these two functions.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=175)** In the case where all of the elements return true for some criteria, both, some, and every are going to return true.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=182)** In the case where none of the elements fit a certain criteria, both some and every are going to return false.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=190)** However, in the in-between case where there are some elements that return true and some that return false, that's where these two functions are going to behave differently, right?
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=201)** Every is going to return false in that situation, some is going to return true.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=206)** Now one last question that you probably have here is whether or not there is a none function, right?
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=213)** Which returns whether or not none of the elements from an array match a given piece of criteria.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=219)** And while there is not a specific non-function, it's actually pretty easy to create, you just have to use numbers.some with the not operator before it and that will tell you if a piece of criteria is true for none of the elements in the array.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=236)** Cool.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-every-and-some-functions?u=76281980&t=237)** So that's the basics of the every and some functions.

>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/preventing-mutation-with-the-slice-function?u=76281980&t=53)** So as an example here, array.sort, this will actually change the order of elements in the array that you call it on.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/preventing-mutation-with-the-slice-function?u=76281980&t=63)** I'll show you an example of this shortly.

> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/preventing-mutation-with-the-slice-function?u=76281980&t=66)** Array.reverse, this will also modify the order of elements in an array.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/preventing-mutation-with-the-slice-function?u=76281980&t=71)** Array.push, this will mutate the array that we call it on by adding another element to it and array.pop, this will also modify the array that it's called on by removing an element from it.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/preventing-mutation-with-the-slice-function?u=76281980&t=86)** And these are just a few of the mutating functions on JavaScript arrays in general.

>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/preventing-mutation-with-the-slice-function?u=76281980&t=97)** So I would definitely recommend testing it if you come across a function that you're not sure of.

>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-sort-function?u=76281980&t=194)** Now the idea of this function that we're specifying here is that it will tell sort whether for any two elements in the array a should come before b.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-sort-function?u=76281980&t=208)** Now the way it does this is by returning a positive value if b should come before a, a negative value if a should come before b, and zero if the two values should be considered equal.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-sort-function?u=76281980&t=226)** So here's what this is going to look like.

>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-sort-function?u=76281980&t=237)** We'll just say return 1.

> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-sort-function?u=76281980&t=240)** Otherwise, we'll say else if a is less than b, then, in ascending order, we want a to come before b, so we're going to return a negative value.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-sort-function?u=76281980&t=252)** Otherwise, if a and b are equal, we're just going to return zero, indicating that they're equal.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/using-the-sort-function?u=76281980&t=258)** So that is how numericalAscending works.

#### Combining array functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/combining-array-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/combining-array-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Okay, well, at this point, we've learned about a number of different declarative functions that can help us manipulate data in a more functional way.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/combining-array-functions?u=76281980&t=9)** So the next thing that we're going to take a look at here is how we can combine these functions to achieve more complex functionality.
>

### 5. 4. Advanced Functional Concepts

#### Currying and partial application
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right.
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=1)** So the next thing that we're going to talk about here, now that we've gone through the basics of data manipulation and first-class functions, we're going to move on to what I would consider to be some more advanced functional concepts.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=13)** And the first one that we're going to talk about here is something called partial application.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=19)** Now, there is a special version of partial application called currying that we'll take a look at shortly.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=25)** But first, let's start with the basics.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=28)** So partial application, in order to demonstrate this concept, what I'm going to do is let's just imagine that we have a simple function called add that takes three numbers.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=41)** This might seem a little bit odd, but you'll see why I'm doing this in a minute.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=44)** And returns the sum of those three numbers, right?
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=49)** Now, calling this function is pretty straightforward.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=52)** You already know how to do it, of course.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=54)** All you do is you say add, and after that you put a pair of parentheses with three arguments inside of it, right?
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=62)** The same number as the number of parameters that we defined the function with.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=66)** So if we run our code here by saying node examples/partial-application.js, sure enough, we see that that gives us the result.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=75)** All right, now, so far, whenever we've seen our functions, and we've seen quite a lot of them in this course, one notable thing about them has been that when we call them, we have to specify all of the arguments in the same time and place.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=93)** In other words, when we want to call our add function, we have to have all three numbers right then and there, ready to pass into it, to get the result, right?
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=104)** Now, that might seem like kind of a given, but there are actually many situations where it would be very beneficial to be able to specify the arguments not all together, right?
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=115)** Either one at a time or in groups.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=118)** So to give you an idea of what this might look like, let me show you what partial application would allow us to do.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=126)** Basically, it would allow us to do things like this.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=129)** It would allow us to call our add function with a single argument, right?
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=135)** One.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=136)** Or two arguments, right?
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=139)** You know, some number of arguments less than the total number of arguments.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=143)** And create a function where those two arguments have been fixed to a given value, right?
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=151)** So we could call this something like const add1 =, and then we call the add function with x equal to 1.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=159)** And then later on we could call add1 with maybe the two remaining arguments, right?
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=165)** That is what partial application allows us to do, right?
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=168)** It allows us to create a sort of pre-configured version of a function, where one or more of the arguments have been fixed to some specific value.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=180)** So to show you what partial application looks like in code and how to actually make it possible, let's take this add function that we have here.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=190)** And what I'm going to do is I'm going to create a new function here called addPartial.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=195)** Now, this addPartial function is going to allow us to partially apply the first argument of the add function, x.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=206)** And here's how it's going to do that.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=207)** addPartial is simply going to be a function that takes one argument.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=213)** And guess what it's going to return?
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=215)** It's going to return another function, which I'll put in another line so that you can kind of see it a little bit better here.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=222)** It's going to return another function that takes the other two arguments.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=227)** And then once we have all three arguments, we're simply going to call add, the original function, with those three arguments that we've accumulated, right?
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=238)** Now, again, this looks a little bit funny, perhaps, at first.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=241)** And maybe it would be better if I put it all on one line, or maybe not.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=245)** But the point here is that we can now call this addPartial function, and we get to pass in the arguments in different times and places in our code.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=258)** So here's how we can use this thing now.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=260)** We can say const add1 = addPartial.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=265)** And then we're going to call that with the first argument, x, set to 1, right?
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=271)** Look what this is doing.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=272)** This is pre configuring this x argument to be 1, so that we only have to specify the other two arguments, right?
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=281)** So let's just go back to there.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=284)** All right, we'll change that back to x and x.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=286)** And now, right, now that we have the add1 function, we can just log out add1 called with any other two arguments, right?
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=295)** We could say 10 and 11, for example.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=297)** And if we run our code, sure enough, that gives us 22.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=302)** 10 plus 11 plus 1, right?
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=306)** So in other words, what's happening here is we're specifying the first argument here, 1 as x, and then when we call the return function, right, this function here with the other two arguments, it has all of the arguments in the same place, and it simply calls whatever function we wanted to partially apply with those three arguments.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=328)** Now, this might seem a little bit abstract right now, but there are actually a lot of different situations where partial application can be helpful.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=336)** For example, let's imagine that we have another function.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=340)** I'm just going to sort of draw a divider line here to keep things separate.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=344)** Let's imagine that we have a function here called something like getPropertyWithDefault.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=351)** And what this does is it takes an object, it takes a property name, we'll just call that key, and then it takes a default value to return if the object doesn't have that key.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=366)** All right? Well, here's what this is going to look like.

>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=377)** Otherwise, what we'll do is we're going to return the default.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=383)** And oops, we need to name that defaultValue here, because default is actually a JavaScript keyword.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=389)** And that's our getPropertyWithDefault function.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=392)** Now, what you can probably imagine is that there are going to be a lot of situations where this getPropertyWithDefault function is going to be called with the same arguments for key and defaultValue, right?
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=407)** So you might have a situation where you want to get, let's say, the 'name' property of different objects with the same defaultValue, right?

> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=425)** And then later on, we do the same thing with a different object, maybe person2, 'name,' 'N/A.'
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=432)** Then, later on, maybe we do the same thing with a third object.

> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=440)** Same thing. 'name' and 'N/A.
>

>

>

>
> **[7:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=463)** We'd have to change this to 'displayName,' this to 'displayName,' this to 'displayName.'
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=469)** You know, we'd have to make that same change in a lot of different places.
>
> **[7:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=472)** Same thing if we wanted to change the default value for all of these things.
>
> **[7:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=477)** Now, here's an alternative.
>
> **[7:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=479)** Because of what we now know about partial application, we can actually just create a partially applied version of this like so.
>
> **[8:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=488)** What we're going to do is we're just going to say const, and we'll say something like getPropertyPartial.
>
> **[8:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=495)** And we get to decide which arguments and in which order they're passed.
>
> **[8:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=498)** So for example, we might want to allow the calling code to specify the key and defaultValue here.
>
> **[8:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=506)** And then we simply have to return another function.
>
> **[8:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=511)** All right?
>
> **[8:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=512)** I'll put that down another line here, again, just to make it a little bit easier to read.
>
> **[8:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=516)** That takes the object and simply calls getPropertyWithDefault with all three of those arguments put together.
>
> **[8:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=526)** All right, so we're going to say object, key, and defaultValue like so.

> **[8:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=531)** And now we can simply create our own getName function automatically by just saying getPropertyPartial and passing 'name' and 'N/A.'
>
> **[9:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=542)** And now we can simply use this getName function in all of these cases instead of having to specify the 'name' and 'N/A' over and over again.

> **[9:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=556)** Because if we want to make a change to, let's say, the 'name' property, here, let me just finish doing that real quick.
>
> **[9:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=562)** getName(building).
>
> **[9:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=564)** If we want to make a change to what the name of this property is, we can just say, you know, 'displayName.'
>
> **[9:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=570)** And if we want to make a change to the default value, we can just change that in one place.
>
> **[9:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=576)** So that is the power of partial application.
>
> **[9:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=579)** And by the way, currying, in case you're wondering about that word, is simply a specific case of partial application where all of the arguments are passed in one by one.
>
> **[9:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=591)** So you end up with something that looks a little bit like this.
>
> **[9:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=593)** And it looks strange, but what it lets you do... Here, let's just change it back to there.
>
> **[9:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=598)** There we go.
>
> **[9:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=599)** What it lets you do is choose exactly how many arguments you want to partially apply at any given point in time, right?
>
> **[10:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=606)** So if we only wanted to specify the 'name' property here, like so, we could just say getPropertyPartial('displayName').
>
> **[10:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=616)** If we then wanted to say getNameWithDefault =.
>
> **[10:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=622)** And then we could say getName, and we provide the defaultValue.
>
> **[10:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=627)** And now we could use getNameWithDefault each time.
>
> **[10:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=633)** All right? So that's the basic idea of currying there.
>
> **[10:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=637)** You can choose whether or not you want to use currying.
>
> **[10:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=639)** I usually stick to using just plain old partial application, like what you see here.
>
> **[10:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=643)** But the choice is up to you.
>
> **[10:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/currying-partial-application?u=76281980&t=645)** So that is the basics of partial application in functional programming.

> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/recursion?u=76281980&t=187)** There's actually two important pieces whenever you're using recursion to solve a problem.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/recursion?u=76281980&t=192)** The first is you need to make sure that there's some sort of change that moves the state of the application or the state of the argument here towards some sort of stopping point or base case where the recursion will end, right?
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/recursion?u=76281980&t=212)** So these two things you really have to have both of them because if you have a change but don't have a stopping point, then well guess what?
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/recursion?u=76281980&t=223)** You're simply going to end up with an application that recursors infinitely.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/recursion?u=76281980&t=227)** And likewise, if you have a stopping point, but no change that moves the function toward that stopping point, you're going to have infinite recursion there, right, because the function's just going to keep calling itself over and over again.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/recursion?u=76281980&t=239)** So that's the basics of recursion.

>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=135)** And what I'm going to do for this is I'm going to copy the Fibonacci function and I'm going to paste it in memoization.js.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=142)** Now, the key concept of memoization here is that whenever a function is called, what we want to do is we want to remember what argument that function was called with and what the result was that the function produced for that argument.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=158)** So in other words, if we know that Fibonacci six is equal to eight, well, every time our Fibonacci function is called with the argument six, from now on, we can simply return eight instead of having to go all the way down the chain as we did before, right?
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=175)** Recursively.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=177)** Alright, so here's how this works.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=178)** What we're actually going to do is we're going to create a mutable object called memo.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=186)** All right? So we'll say let memo.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=188)** And what this object is going to contain is the keys, which are going to be the arguments that this Fibonacci function was called with.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=197)** And the value for that key is going to be the result that that function produced for that argument.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=205)** So we would have, you know, zero, one, one, one, and then we would have two that would produce two, three would produce three, four would produce five, right?
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=217)** Because two plus three, you see where we're going with this.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=220)** And then we would have five would produce eight, six would produce 13, and so on, so forth.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=229)** Now, what our Fibonacci function is going to do is it's going to check to see if a given argument is already in this memo object.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=238)** And if it is, it's simply going to return that value without calculating it itself.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=244)** If it's not, it's going to figure out the answer for that argument and insert it into this memo object.
>

>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=267)** And so for now, we're just going to be comfortable with modifying this thing.

> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=330)** So we're going to save that result essentially, and now we're going to return the result just like we did before.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=336)** So now that we've done this, now that we've memorized our Fibonacci function, we should be able to call it with much higher numbers.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=345)** And because we're remembering the result when that was produced, it's going to go much faster this time.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=353)** All right? So we're going to say fib, we'll do 100 this time just to you that same number and how much faster it's going to go with this.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=360)** And if we run our code by saying node examples slash memoization.js, sure enough, we see that that is able to calculate that very large number much faster because instead of having to recalculate, you know, fib 99 or fib 98 or 97 multiple times as it goes down the chain, it only has to calculate each of those once, right?
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=385)** Because after it calculates it once, it simply returns the result that it got the previous time so that future calls can simply look that up.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-functional-programming-with-javascript-es6-plus-24300838/memoization?u=76281980&t=395)** So anyway, that is the basics of memorization in functional programming and how it can help us with certain functions like this recursive Fibonacci function.
